Chapter 18

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

In the end, Agatha and the boys reached an agreement: she and Rio would be staying in the apartment indefinitely, and Agatha would consult on the decoration of their next two properties. 

They all concurred that it was a very fair and reasonable situation, certainly nothing requiring police intervention—Tony couldn’t even remember why they’d gone to the precinct in the first place—and, by the end, they all went out for lunch martinis.

“You are seriously evil,” Rio chuckled as she sipped a daiquiri through a straw. The boys were in the bathroom, leaving them alone with six empty glasses and a full bowl of pad thai. It was happy hour at Thai-lai, and Andrew and Tony had been happy to pay for all four of them. “Celebrating our new interior designer,” Tony had said, and they all clinked glasses.

Rio had been trying her best not to laugh in their faces.

Agatha batted her eyelashes up at her. “Oh, I’m evil because I’m doing what a girl needs to do to survive? How reductionist.”

“To survive?” Rio laughed. “You mean three shots of watermelon vodka, two daiquiris, three entrees, two bowls of popcorn shrimp—”

“Can I get anything else for your ladies?”

A waiter had snuck up behind them with a bright, shining smile that said, Thank you for spending way too much money at our restaurant, would you like to spend even more?

Agatha was as quick as a whip. “Two more daiquiris, please.”

The waiter nodded while Rio’s mouth hung open. “Of course.”

He puttered away, and Rio snorted in disbelief.

“Unbelievable.”

“The shrimp? I know.”

Agatha popped another of the crustaceans in her mouth, crunching down on the batter as she grinned up at Rio. Agatha was smiling at her in that way that made Rio feel like she could spend the rest of her life just like this, drinking watered down alcohol and not paying for it. 

And for a flicker of a moment—it almost looked like Agatha was thinking the same thing, her smile turning soft, the barely-there wrinkles around her eyes bunching together.

Rio forgot herself in the feeling, and lifted her hand to Agatha’s face, tucking a hair behind her ear, and saying nothing. The dimly lit restaurant was covered in these stupid RGB lights—blinking red, green, blue, red, green, blue—and they danced on Agatha’s lips like a Christmas tree. Rio so badly wanted to kiss the holiday sheen off that pretty mouth.

“You’re gorgeous,” Rio whispered, without really meaning to.

Agatha’s cheeks colored this unbelievably pretty pink. She had never seen Agatha blush, not before they started doing all of… this. Whatever this was.

But she was addicted to it now. She loved the rush that came with knowing she was responsible for making Agatha feel that way.

“And you’re drunk,” Agatha replied, setting down her snack. She didn’t move away, though—her eyes still pinned to Rio’s own, that easy smile still on her lips. “Rio…”

“What?” Rio frowned. Because she felt like she was about to get chastised. Then she added petulantly, and yes, drunkenly, “I can’t call you gorgeous now?” Is that another rule?

Listening to the words come out of her mouth, it occurred to her how many times over the years she had called Agatha gorgeous. But how many of those times had she meant it like she did now? It was so strange—it was almost like the memories had a veil over them.

She could remember the instances—Agatha looking pretty for a Venetian ball, Agatha shuffling across a beach at dusk—but she couldn’t remember the emotion she’d felt each time. Like a photo without color.

Agatha laughed coldly, and she picked Rio’s fingers up where they lay, still, on the side of her temple, placing them back onto the table. Which was obviously a rejection, Rio wasn’t so dense that she couldn’t see that, but the strange thing is that Agatha didn’t let go of her hand when she set it down. 

She just laid her own palm over Rio’s, and pretended like she didn’t.

“Sure you can,” Agatha said coolly. Her effortless front had returned. Then, below the table, she slipped her heeled foot around Rio’s ankle. “But don’t do it somewhere where I can’t do anything about it.”

And oh—Rio’s mind was suddenly as blank as a sheet of paper. She really wasn’t proud of how much obvious evasiveness she was letting Agatha get away with, but she couldn’t help it.

A quiet, desperate feeling in her chest told her that she only had so many of these opportunities left. That there was a window to all this, and if she didn’t jump at every offered bone now—

So she sprung out of her seat like a dog who’d heard the treat jar shake, and towered over Agatha, who looked up at her with dark pupils.

“Let’s go,” Rio said breathlessly.

Agatha laughed in surprise. “Really? I mean—just leave them here, with the bill? While we bite each other silly back at their apartment?”

Rio was already getting light headed thinking about it. “Pretty much.”

Agatha grinned at her fondly. It made Rio’s heart clench.

“You really do spend too much time with me.”

She took Rio’s hand, and let her help her up.

It sounded light and meaningless when Rio replied, “Every moment I can get.”



***



They didn’t even make it through the apartment door before Rio had Agatha pressed up against the cold hallway wall, her hand clutching at the waistline of the other woman’s pants.

Agatha tasted like strawberry alcohol—sweet, too sweet, the kind of sweetness that hides something sharp and dangerous—and Rio’s swollen mind could only think, repeatedly, I love you, I love you, I love you, as she pressed open-mouth kisses to the other woman’s throat.

“Don’t tease me,” Agatha whined. “I want to feel your teeth.”

Rio groaned out some obscene mix of syllables, because what else was she supposed to do when Agatha said things like that. She let her fangs fall forward, graze on alabaster skin—

“Wow. Wow. I mean, I want to say I’m surprised, but I totally called that.”

A voice that belonged to neither of them echoed from a few feet away. Surprise hit Rio and Agatha like a whip and they bolted apart. Rio immediately rescinded her fangs, grateful that she hadn’t drawn any blood when she saw just who was waiting for them, standing like an awkward pin needle with his hands shoved nervously in his pockets.

“Billy? ” Rio mumbled.

“Jesus,” Agatha groaned. “Does our apartment have some kind of twink bat signal I don’t know about?”

Billy grumbled. “You can’t just call people twinks, Agnes. I don’t go by any label—”

Agatha waved her hand, and turned toward the apartment door, searching her pockets for the keys. “This argument hasn’t even started and I’m already over it. Goodbye, teenager.”

“Wait. Stop. Also, I’m twenty three—”

Then, like the third train to smash into this mess of a conversation, Rio mumbled, “Wait, you called what? ” She was still blinking stupidly as she processed the first thing he’d said. 

Billy narrowed his eyes as Agatha pushed the door open and waltzed inside. “You and Agnes? Being… a thing?” he said, gesturing to the wall that Rio had pressed Agatha against moments before. “I mean, you literally dug a peephole into the fabric of our shared cubicle with a pencil, like some kind of mole rat, so you could spy on her in the meeting room across the hall.”

“Oh my god. You did what?” Agatha laughed cruelly from inside the apartment. Rio burned red, and followed the other woman’s lead into the living room. Billy looked strangely at the now empty doorway before sighing and following them in, closing it firmly behind himself.

“Oh, yes, just make yourself at home,” Agatha muttered, rolling her eyes and putting on the coffee machine.

Her movements were still drunk and clumsy, and she missed the button several times while trying to press it. It made Rio smile despite herself.

“Gosh, you really are a goner,” Billy said as he took a single glance at Rio’s face. He then neatly removed his shoes, and instead of doing something normal, like sitting on the couch, he continued to stand in place and play with the ring on his finger. But his awkwardness did not prevent him from playing private investigator. “So, how long have the two of you been married?”

“Married?” Rio said hoarsely.

“Ten years,” said Agatha, without missing a beat. “Not that it’s any of your business.”

“Ten years?” Rio said, more hoarsely.

What the fuck was Agatha talking about? What the fuck was going on?

Hot coffee burbled out of their Keurig machine and into Agatha’s beloved mug—a bright pink ceramic that said If you can’t beat them, eat them—and Agatha whisked it away and wandered over to the couch, where she dragged Rio down next to her, basically onto her lap.

Rio gawked at her. But Agatha didn’t give her an inch, her face turned to Billy.

Is this about the funeral? It had to be about the funeral.

She knew Agatha had been preparing some kind of character for them to play that involved them being married, but the roles were only supposed to extend as far as getting through the gates and inside the venue.

Billy wasn’t even supposed to know about their aliases. They were planning on avoiding him during as much of the service as possible, and if he did approach them with any questions, they’d just Suggest them away from his mousey little brain. But apparently some part of that plan had changed. Maybe it had changed just now, inside Agatha’s mind. Rio had no idea.

To help relieve some of that cluelessness, she looked up at Billy and asked, “Um, no offense, but what are you doing in our apartment?”

“Oh,” he frowned. “I thought that was obvious.”

Rio gave him a look that said It absolutely is not.

“I think I figured out who killed Tommy.”

If the Oscars had installed cameras inside their apartment at that moment, Agatha and Rio would have swept every category.

(Vampires historically hold the most awards for Best Actor and Best Actress. Lauren Graham herself holds twenty-seven awards, which she’s received as several different people. Most famously as Katharine Hepburn.)

Agatha’s fingers scrunched around the fabric of Rio’s pants, but besides that, everything else about her stayed still. After a moment, she laughed.

“Oh yeah, Sherlock?” She taunted, leaning forward. “And who do you think did it?”

The confidence in Billy’s shoulders deflated a little at Agatha’s condescension, but not completely. With a huff, he finally sat down, sliding his lanky body into the eggshell armchair that sat next to the television. It swallowed him up, making him look like a grape in the palm of a giant’s hand. Hardly a threat.

Still, she felt Agatha tighten her grasp on her knee when he came closer—the faintest evidence of nerves—and Rio’s heart swelled that she was what Agatha held onto when she felt scared. Like an anchor.

(A totally stupid thought to linger on when they were potentially about to be accused of murder, but Rio had never been great at prioritizing.)

“My step-brother was caught up in a lot of…” Billy’s leg bobbed nervously. “Well, he…”

Billy made a bunch of pathetic hand gestures that said nothing. His leg bobbed even more.

“Dear god. Out with it,” Agatha muttered. “Which is it? Lobbyists? Mafia? Slave traders?”

His leg stopped bobbing when he gasped. “ Slave traders?” Billy looked positively scandalized. “I mean Tommy was a dickhead, but I can’t believe you’d even suggest that.”

Agatha pursed her lips and Rio could tell, from a single look, that she wanted to say I’ve been alive longer than the Atlantic slave trade, I’ve watched the CIA topple governments—and entire nations—on a whim, and I know just how many things powerful people get away with if they have a good enough public relations team, so yes, it wouldn’t be so surprising, but in a truly uncharacteristic show of self-regulation, she just shrugged.

“He was in a lot of debt,” Billy revealed finally, to which Agatha full body gasped , replying “the son of a billionaire? In debt? ” like the know-it-all asshole she was, and Billy believed it for only a moment before he realized she was just being herself, and continued on, “My step-dad didn’t know about it. But I looked through a bunch of files—” Billy reached for the knapsack he was wearing, and began extracting a series of manila folders. 

“—And Tommy’s company, the company we all worked at, was failing miserably.” He set the folders down on the table, and Agatha, feigning disinterest, picked them up and began to skim apathetically. “So he kept inflating the bottom line by buying and selling some sort of… financial assets… I don’t know, it’s all so vague , but he was selling them to a bunch of high-rolling buyers around the world. In several locations. Beijing, Tokyo…”

“And Vienna and Bucharest,” Agatha mumbled, reading off the file. Rio’s eyebrow arched, seeing the way something had activated inside the other woman. Agatha had that singular, burning focus about her suddenly, but it flickered out almost as soon as it’d come. She tossed the folder onto the table. “So, what? You think his loan sharks killed him?”

Billy bit down on his lip and stared at his lap for a moment.

When his head raised again, a chill entered his voice.

“It was either them, or my step-dad.”

Then Agatha snorted.

“Oh my god, you think Daddy Dearest killed his inept son?”

“Maybe,” Billy huffed, throwing his hands up. “I don’t know. These were the only files I could get from his office before the police arrived and whisked everything away. But it wouldn’t surprise me. My step-dad never seemed to care about Tommy. Or me. Or Mom. He was so—he was so strange. Like, vacant. He’d switch emotions on a whim, happy to sad to angry, and then not even seem to remember why he’d been in a mood a few minutes ago. So no. I wouldn’t be surprised if he did it. I wouldn’t be surprised if he didn’t even remember that he did it.”

Agatha stilled next to her, her shoulders going rigid.

It wasn’t hard to imagine why.

Everything in Billy’s description was accurate to the behavior of a thrall. Which meant that he had been—and likely still was—under Wanda’s thumb. 

Which meant that killing Wanda would be the only way to claim him as their own.

Which Rio hadn’t really had a problem with, not when it was so… theoretical.

But talking to Wanda’s son—sitting across from him like this, observing him in all his adolescent awkward squirminess—the idea sat differently in her chest now. Her stomach turned uncomfortably.

“Billy,” Agatha said slowly, licking her lips. “Why are you talking to us about this?”

Billy blinked. He narrowed his eyes, giving them a look again that said Isn’t it obvious?

“Well. No offense, but you’re the two biggest bullshitters I’ve ever met,” he said. “Well, at least she is.” He gestured toward Agatha, and Rio didn’t take offense. “I’m not good at lying. STYRIA - 1401

 

The morning in which Agatha’s life changed irrevocably was completely unremarkable in all ways except one. Summer was breaking over Styria in the year 1401, pink foxglove flowers were sprouting into poisonous life off the cliffside of Riegersburg Castle, and Agatha Harkness, the gargoyle that she was, was welcoming in the fifteenth century from her stoop, hiding from her mother, sipping stolen mead, and partaking in her favorite activity.

Watching, and, better yet, judging.

The flowers, and everything else—the common people, far below in the fields, with their horse-dirtied tunics tucked into belts; the snobby nobles, women in floor-length gowns, slogging up the castle’s hills in carriages, and worst of all, the members of the Harkness clan, immediately recognizable from their black cloaks, black skirts, black hats, all in the summer heat.

They were collected like a wasp’s nest in the castle courtyard, drinking ‘wine,’ as they were told to refer to it as, and ‘entertaining’ a group of nobles from a far away place.

They did this every month or two. A group of well-dressed people would arrive from quite some distance, the clan would invite them for an innocent soiree, business dealings would be made, and the group would leave, none the wiser that they were now thralls—red eyed and mindless.

She’d seen the pattern so many times that looking at it all bored her to death. She was just about to finish the rest of the alcohol and tuck into a book when something jumped out to her. Not literally—nothing got this far up, save one of mother’s hawks—but far below, in the courtyard. There was a stranger there. Undoubtedly. Not a stranger as in one of the mortal strangers, one of the victims, but a vampire stranger. One of their own, except not.

Agatha’s heart stuttered, and she felt an emotion that she hadn’t in many years. Excitement.

She’d felt its cousin—fear—many times over, everyday, but this one felt new in her chest.

Almost as if sensing her, the stranger looked up.

Brown eyes peered out. Big, docile ones. Like a deer. Woman’s eyes. And woman’s skin. Tanned, like soft leather. It was rare to see a complexion like that in the valley. The mixture of it all was as captivating to Agatha as watching a bird devour a mouse.

So when she heard the rapping of knuckles against metal, Agatha whisked it open, just a crack. Enough for the guest to see Agatha and for Agatha to see the guest, but for no one in the ballroom to get a good look.

Oh, wow.

The stranger was even prettier at this distance.

She was wearing a snugly fitting black doublet over a dark green houppelande, its long, hanging sleeves dripping toward the grass. Around her middle was a black, jeweled belt, with a sheath, for what looked like a dagger. 

A dagger.

The woman was carrying a weapon. And making no effort to conceal it, either.

It sent a shiver down Agatha’s spine. Fear, but also, undoubtedly—

“Gott zum Gruße,” the woman said quietly, her accent curling delightfully around the ß. The greeting was standard, but not nearly as polite as the Harkness family would typically require. The doorman would have turned her away six times by now. The red flags were piling up in spades.

Automatically, Agatha replied, “Seid mir gegrüßt, edle Dame.”

The young woman’s mouth snickered in a way that made Agatha’s stomach turn. She so rarely saw women allow their lips to make expressions that were so… opinionated.

Noble women were supposed to keep their mouths in obedient little lines. Emotions were better concealed that way, secrets more easily protected. That’s what women were, secret keepers.

Yet, on the contrary, it looked as if this woman was mocking her. Openly so.

It was completely enchanting. 

“Noble lady?” The stranger chuckled. “Please, don’t call me that. It’s a lie.”

The stranger’s smirk faltered in response, caught off guard now that she could see Agatha in her full glory—black dress wrapped around a tight waist, unbrushed brown hair falling in chaotic tufts around her shoulders. 

She finds me pretty, was the story Agatha’s oversized ego told herself. When the stranger’s eyes dropped briefly down to her chest, Agatha couldn’t help but grin in that wolfish way her mother would slap her for. Scratch that. She finds me irresistible.

Okay, perhaps that was a push too far. She knew she was at least partially inventing fantasies. Even if she herself was born with a penchant for the same sex, she guessed that few other women shared her proclivity—or none, if you were to ask her mother.

“May I ask you a question?” the stranger said, and her voice had gone all quiet. 

Agatha’s answer to that question, she would realize much later, was the singular thing that made the difference between her living and dying that night.

But in that moment death was about the farthest thing from her mind.

She leaned up against the doorframe and said something entirely stupid instead, “Anything you’d like, gorgeous.”

Agatha frowned. There was nothing erotic about that man. Her mood soured even darker when she considered that maybe this woman was not only uninterested in her, but here to court a different member of the Harkness family. 

“Are you in search of him?”

The woman seemed to consider how to answer. “Yes,” she said. “I am.”

“Why?”

“I have business with him.”

“Well.” Agatha huffed. “I do hope that business is murder, because any other business would be a dreadful mistake on your behalf. He’s an oaf and a predator. If you marry him, he’ll drink himself into a daze before finding a lady of the night to squander in your bedsheets. His balding head belongs under a guillotine. But yes, he is here.”

The woman’s eyes widened in what seemed like surprise, or maybe even terror, which only made Agatha more further disappointed. This stranger carried a dagger, for Hell’s sake. A little joke about death shouldn’t have even moved her.

At this point she was only kidding — disappointment made her say mean, ridiculous things — but the stranger’s answer was as fast as a whip crack.

“I promise.” She sounded almost eager. Her tongue was swiping along her lips. “I promise I will kill him and if you don’t mind, I intend to do away with the rest of the House, as well.”

Agatha froze. Her fingers pressed into the cold metal of the door and she just studied the woman for a moment, expecting her to backpedal on such an insane statement, but she didn’t. She looked at Agatha as if she meant it. Truly meant it.

And Agatha believed her.

And for a moment, Agatha imagined a future in which it was true. A version of her life that was lived in the way she intended it, free of Evanora’s influence. Because as long as Evanora was alive, she would corral Agatha like cattle. She would ensure Agatha was a stranger in every city, unwelcome at every inn, like a dog herding sheep back to the den, she would ensure that Agatha never left her sight — never made good on any of her aspirations.

It was with great offense that Agatha realized she wasn’t wrong.

Beyond her initial attraction, there was something about this woman — most likely her threat to murder her mother, which was always an endearing quality in a potential friend — that felt like meeting a twin flame. Perhaps it was her unusual accent, the way it colored German to make it sound pretty in a way that Agatha never thought it could, or her straight-forward way of asking questions, or, perhaps it was just that they were both strange. 

All her life Agatha had felt like an outlier. A stubborn stain in the carpet of the 15th century. It was bizarre and disorienting to find in front of her someone who existed equally in her wrongness. A second stain, resistant, refusing to bleed out into the wash.

But if Agatha wasn’t stubborn, she’d simply die.  “Of course I’m not.”

The woman laughed, as if she didn’t believe her. “Well then.” She put her hand on the door. “In that case, I see no reason for you to not let me inside.”

“Not without a name. I want to know who it is I will be thanking when it’s over.”

The woman bit down on her lip, then shook her head. “You are quite unbelievable.”

“So I’ve been told. Now, out with it.”

The woman laughed again, and Agatha was beginning to grow all too used to the sound. It would be a real pity when she was soon dead.

“Rio.” She seemed almost sheepish when she said it, which Agatha immediately frowned at. Because it was a beautiful name. Delightfully short, so much shorter than the terrible monikers that filled the road signs and the plaques of this dreadful country. “Rio Vidal.”

Agatha hummed, then, with reluctance, returned the favor, “Agatha Harkness.”

The wind blew shrilly past both of their ears, and Agatha could hear shouts from inside for her to close the door, for fear of a draft. This little perfect bubble of theirs would be popped soon, and Rio would enter, and perhaps she’d try and kill her family, and most definitely she would die. But in that moment, at least she was alive, and at least Agatha could have her for a moment.

“I think we’re going to be friends, Agatha Harkness.”

Agatha laughed, and turned her head toward the party. “You’d be the first to ever try.”

Chapter 22

If Rio thought being Agatha’s wife in bed was torture enough, nothing — not solitary confinement, not years in federal prison, not being sliced open and knit back together with a sewing needle — prepared her for the way she’d feel when she woke up the next day, and Agatha hadn’t taken the wedding ring off.

Not even during breakfast, her hands messy with olive oil, or in the shower, her hands wringing wet hair dry.

Not once.

She wants to keep it on, her foolish mind told her.

Or she can’t be arsed to take it off.

The funeral was a few hours away, after all.

Rio propped her elbows up on kitchen island as Agatha rounded the corner from the bathroom, hot, humid steam wafting in, clouding the kitchen like the view outside an airplane window.

Her roommate (or, in the dark crevices of her wishful mind, her wife) was more stunning than Cleopatra in just her towel, dew droplets of water collected on her bruised, bitten shoulders.

She looks so gorgeous like that.

Covered in bite marks. 

Agatha failed to notice her there, so Rio watched her silently, like the moon watches the sun, as Agatha plugged in their coffee machine, and toyed with the edge of her ring as it booted up.

Agatha’s lips twitched up into a sly smile.

“I can feel you watching me, you know.” 

Rio startled, blushing.

She swore she hadn’t noticed her.

“I wasn’t watching you.”

Liar, liar, liar.

“Right, just like you didn’t kill Tommy Ashcroft,” Agatha chuckled, amused, as she slowly punched in an elaborate amount of custom settings into the feeble Keurig machine, which whined like a baby at the terror she was inflicting on it. After a moment, she looked up, toward the fridge, then frowned gravely, as if disgusted. “God, I caught sight of the thing again. Are you sure we can’t burn it?”

  The thing was a portrait of Andrew and Tony’s cocker spaniel that looked like it was painted by someone going through a psychotic episode — its eyes were the size of golf balls, and the color palette was red and pink and yellow.

 Agatha had hidden it partially behind the refrigerator, but you could still see the creature’s snout poking out if you caught it from the right angle. And she had apparently found that angle.

Rio was just grateful for the change in topic. Anything to distract from the fact that she had woken up with Agatha’s arm slung tightly around her stomach, Agatha’s mouth pressed in a barely-there kiss to her neck. 

Anything to prevent the creeping feeling that something was building between them, like a boulder might teeter at the top of the cliff, before ultimately diving into a chasm.

She turned the corner around the island, then leaned casually against the kitchen counter, so they were facing each other, trying to project confidence she didn’t have.

Rio laughed sharply. “ Twink? You are getting way too much mileage out of a word that you absolutely could not define if I asked you to.”

“Of course I could define it.” The coffee burbled noisily as Agatha licked her lips, staring at Rio as if she might find the answer in her face. “A homosexual man who is… in my business.”

Rio wanted to kiss the stupidity out of this woman. “Absolutely not.”

Apparently Agatha was thinking the same thing, because her eyes dipped down to Rio’s lips, and she bit down on her own. 

Rio’s chest clenched, and last night replayed like shots of film in her head.

“You should listen to me less.”

They pair of them just stared, and stared, neither of them moving. The coffee began to pour into the drip tray, but there was no mug there to catch it. Agatha didn’t seem to notice. Rio could hear the other woman’s heartbeat in her throat.

“I can kiss you? Outside of sex?”

“Whenever you want.”

Whenever you want. 

That was permission, wasn’t it?

As close to a get out of jail free card as Agatha could give her.

But because Rio was good, too good, and cowardly, too cowardly — she decided to give her an out. One chance to backpedal. 

“Your coffee’s spilling,” Rio said. Because it was.

Agatha’s eyebrows rose ever-slightly, but she didn’t look away from Rio’s gaze. “Oh, is it?”

Neither of them moved an inch, the tension like a taut rope. Rio looked at the marks of her own teeth on Agatha’s neck; the imprints of her nails on Agatha’s exposed collarbones.

And because Rio was still Rio, she couldn’t help herself.

She warned her, “I’m going to kiss you again.”

And Agatha looked at her as if she was the most ridiculous person on Earth. “Well, I fucking hope so. Or else I just wasted a perfectly good latte.”

Rio’s eyes widened. But before Agatha could tease her more, Rio shoved the Keurig to the side, the machine wheezing against the granite, coffee spitting onto the floor, and lifted Agatha up onto the kitchen counter. The other woman let out a gasp, and Rio quieted it with her mouth, the kiss harder than she’d meant it to be, more forceful than she’d intended —

“Sorry.” Her words were messy and smudged against Agatha’s incessant lips.

Agatha’s shower damp thighs curled around Rio’s sweatpants. “Don’t.”

Rio withdrew, breathing slowly. Agatha’s pupils were already blown. Her hair already a mess even though Rio had barely raked her hands through it. The perfect picture of desire. Like last night hadn’t ended at all. Like they were right back there, Rio’s fingers curling inside of her.

But I want more.

The realization toppled over her like a ton of bricks. She no longer felt satisfied to settle for what a week ago would be more than enough. Like a naive addict, she’d ridden the hedonic treadmill to the top of the hill, and now she was greedy and desperate at the top of the cliff.

Because the longer Rio looked at Agatha, the more she wanted to pull away completely. Not because she didn’t want this, the inside of her thighs already told her otherwise, but for the opposite reason — 

She wanted so desperately for them to just be able to kiss for the sake of kissing. She wanted them to kiss like it was punctuation; kiss and then eggs, bacon, waffles; kiss and then get dressed; kiss and then go on with their day. Kiss hello, goodbye, good morning. 

Kiss like mortals do.

But then Agatha said, breathless, nails scratching into Rio’s shoulders, “Fuck me.”

And Rio blinked. Re-evaluated.

She was as weak as she was anemic.

“Are you sure? The granite might be kind of —”

“I don’t care.”

“But the funeral —”

Ignoring her, Agatha asked quietly, “Do you still have the wedding ring on?”

Their eyes met. Rio’s heart clenched.

“Yes,” she breathed.

Rio watched Agatha’s throat bob. Like she was swallowing something down.

“Fuck me with it one more time,” she said. “Before this is all over.”

Before this is all over felt like a knife, serrated, cutting into her and leaving nothing left. But Agatha was starting to open her legs, the towel parting at her abdomen, and she looked like every goddess humanity had dreamt of, and smelled like her shower oil. Olympian pink.

And who was Rio to refuse Aphrodite?

By miracle, they still managed to leave the house on time.

Agatha hired an Uber Black, and it fit right in with the procession of darkened limousines crawling across the streets towards the funeral. They borrowed two black suits from Zara minutes before meeting their driver, clipping the tags out in the backseat as alarms rang back at the mall.

“Forty-two dollars?” Agatha scoffed, holding the limp white paper in her hand. “What a ripoff. This isn’t even real cashmere.”

“Is it really a ripoff if we didn’t pay for it?”

“It’s about the principal of it, Rio.”

Rio laughed, and she was grateful to laugh — because if she wasn’t laughing, she’d be dwelling. Dwelling on the fact that Agatha couldn’t seem to kiss her without fucking her afterwards. Which made sense, of course, because that’s all this was. Fucking. All this was for Agatha, at least. What this was for Rio was a slow, torturous death.

A slow death that involved fucking Agatha until the countertop chipped, and the walls shook, and that terrible painting fell to the floor, behind the fridge, where they could never find it out again; until she was panting in Agatha’s ears, “ come for me, come for me, come on the ring, baby, I know you want to be mine, show me what that looks like, ” and it was those last words that did it — Agatha careening over the edge as silver slipped inside of her.

And for as much as Agatha was the actress between them, it was Rio who felt like the real performer as time went on. Rio the one pretending like she still cared about this plan, about the Ashcrofts; cared about having money, or even a place to live. She felt like a liar through and through, a method actress auditioning for a movie without a name.

Is this how Agatha feels all the time?

That harrowing idea carried her through the suburbs, bright green tree canopies flying by, summer in its full, resplendent blush. 

Everything looked so joyous. Rio felt like a walking corpse on its way to a funeral. 

Death consuming her thoughts, her mind drifted, for the first time that morning, to the plan.

“Are we still planning on —” She took note of the driver, and decided to soften her wording. “ — taking care of Wanda?”

Agatha rapped her perfectly-red acrylics on the side of the car before turning to face her with a sly expression. “Why? Are you getting soft on me?”

Rio’s gut twisted unpleasantly. She took those words in a way that she knew Agatha didn’t mean them. But she felt embarrassed anyway.

“No.” She looked out the window so she didn’t have to face Agatha, or by association, her emotions. But she ended up ramming headfirst into them anyway – “But I do sort of feel bad for the kid. I don’t exactly take pleasure in taking away someone’s mother.” She paused, and emotion lodged uncomfortably in her throat. “He’s not even a vampire. He doesn’t understand this world.”

Like herself, those many years ago. Before she’d been dragged by the teeth into immortality.

Agatha didn’t reply for a moment, and Rio worried she might have said something to anger her, but then she felt a soft hand clasp around her own.

“I’d never force you to do something you don’t want to do,” Agatha said quietly, almost sweetly, and Rio felt her heart flutter. But then, because Agatha was still Agatha, she added, “Even if Wanda has a very killable face.”

Rio smiled despite herself. “I believe the expression is punchable, Agatha.”

Agatha waved her off. “Language evolves.”

Rio chuckled. “Does it evolve, actually? Or do you just strangle it into submission?”

Per usual, that was only half a joke — Agatha had spent the better part of the 17th century Suggesting peasants into learning completely made-up vocabulary. Fopdoodle was her proudest work. Well, her proudest work until she eventually replaced it with nincompoop .

“Hm. If last night was anything to go by,” Agatha squeezed her hand, then said breathily, “Strangling things into submission feels like your forte, not mine. Wifey.”

It took everything inside Rio to prevent her mouth from falling clean open. This woman will eventually kill me. This woman will eventually kill me, and I’ll have seen it coming the entire way, until the knife is in my mouth, and I’m thanking her for putting it there. The driver cleared his throat loudly, and turned up the music. Agatha smiled like a witch.

Outside the window, the cathedral began to crest over the horizon. Hundreds of well-dressed guests peppered courtyards protected by tall stone fencing.

“Well. If we’re not going to take the Wanda route,” Agatha said, effortlessly gliding through topics as if she hadn’t just sent a full-body shiver down Rio’s spine. “We’re going to have to come up with an alternative way to deal with Ashcroft.”

Rio eyed the GPS, then the traffic in front of them. The street was clogged like cholesterol in arteries. “Well, we have two minutes until we arrive. You’ve carried out a coup d'etat in less time. So, chop chop.”

Rio could see Agatha’s brilliant smile in the reflection of the window, and she couldn’t resist anymore, turning to look at her — she was nothing if not a naive fish to a worm, ready as ever to wrap her mouth around the hook. Especially when that hook was as pleasant to look at as Agatha Harkness, suit blouse ruggedly undone on her collarbones, wild hair tamed with a sweet-smelling curl cream.

Agatha hummed. “Thirty seconds is all I need.”

“Oh, now we’re getting cocky.”

“It’s not cocky if it’s true. Put on a timer.”

“I’m not putting on a timer.”

Agatha pouted. “Hey Siri, put on a timer—”

Rio’s phone began to vibrate in her pocket. “You’re so—”

“—put on a timer for thirty seconds.”

“I’ve set a timer for thirty seconds,” Rio’s phone, the traitor, responded.

“Thank you Siri,” Agatha said, giving Rio a shit-eating grin, then brought her leg up into the seat, wrapping her arm around it so her chin could sit on her kneecap, as she often did when she needed to think hard on something quickly — it was devastatingly endearing. “Well, if he is her thrall, then the only other way to sever their connection is to put as much distance between them as possible. It’ll make it a lot easier for me to unwind his thoughts that way.”

“That’s where Billy comes in.”

“The kid?”

“Mm. We tell him we have a lead in the case,” Agatha snorted. Because she was, well, evil. “But it requires us talking to Daddy Dearest. He’ll introduce us to the big man, get us past his security, then I think — hmm — oh. Oh, oh god. That’s good. God, I’m good.”

Rio rolled her eyes. “What is it?”

The timer went off the same time that the door opened on Agatha’s side. The driver turned his head to them, and with the most grim expression Rio had ever seen, said, “Please leave.”

Agatha laughed, then tugged Rio by the hand out into the broad sunlight, and as the rays stung her skin, Rio wasn’t sure what pain was worse — the bright, eviscerating light, or knowing Agatha would eventually let go of her.

For the fifth time in fifteen minutes, Agatha was sobbing.

Which, normally – Rio would be concerned. Very concerned.

But this was Agatha Harkness. And Agatha Harkness, when placed in a situation where she could make fun of as many people as possible, could simply not resist the pleasure.

“He was so,” Agatha sniffled. “So kind. Such kind eyes. Those little baby blues.”

Agatha’s current victim, or rather, victims, were a nicely dressed lesbian couple. An asian woman in a suit and high heels, and a black woman wearing a form-fitting green dress. They had introduced themselves as Alice and Jen, but Rio was positive Agatha had no idea what their names were, and would continue to not know, even if they repeated them to her.

“...And how did you know the deceased?” Alice asked softly.

“Oh, you know.” Agatha wiped her eyes, which were flush with tears that Rio frankly did not understand how she could produce on command. “Support group.”

“Support group?” Jen said. “Support for… what?”

Agatha cleared her throat. Which Rio knew was a sign that she was about to say something incredibly stupid.

“Porn addiction,” Agatha said solemnly. “It’s what killed him in the end.”

Yep. Bullseye.

“Luckily,” Agatha sighed. “My wife has been incredibly supportive.”

She felt Agatha’s hand tighten around her waist, but no matter how desperate Rio was, she was not desperate enough to play a prop in this joke.

“Sorry. You’ll have to excuse her,” Rio said, smiling tightly. “She’s diagnosed with chronic attention-deficit disorder. As in, she constantly needs attention, or she’ll die.”

Agatha pouted, as if to say You’re no fun.  

Alice and Jen just stared at them, speechless.

“We’ll be leaving now,” Rio said. “Nice to meet you both.”

Jen squinted. “Nice to – uh – sure.”

They’d been lingering at the outskirts of the party for too long, and Rio was growing desperate for this whole charade to end. Human funerals were no fun. Everyone was so miserable, reciting the same bland stories about a man they could have cared less about while he was alive, and worst of all – no one let you inspect the corpse.

Agatha protested, “Sue me for keeping myself entertained. Rich people are so boring. I can only talk about stocks for so long without wanting to pull someone’s eyes out.”

The other woman swallowed it down in two gulps, then tossed it to the grass, like they were at a college rager, not a funeral for the son of a billionaire. They received several looks.

And truthfully, it wasn’t that Rio was annoyed. There was nothing she enjoyed more than watching Agatha bother people. It was more that she was… concerned? Because even when Agatha got like this, she still knew how to keep the attention off her. 

But she was acting… messy.

His teeth — fangs — were exposed. His eyes were bright red. He was tall, so tall.

It was him again. The man from far away. He should be dead.  He had tricked her parents into letting him stay. He had prayed on their charity. I killed him. I watched him die.

“It made me feel better.” Say all of it. “Kissing you… makes me feel better.”

Agatha swallowed roughly. Rio could hear her heartbeat pick up relentlessly in her neck. When she spoke, her voice was small. Like it barely escaped her throat at all.

“It makes me feel better, too.”

Rio bit down on her lip, feeling blood pump deliriously in her veins, making her feel insane and naive and optimistic — because maybe this was the cliff, and maybe she was teetering precariously above it, but maybe Agatha was the chasm, calling her name with open arms, open hands, maybe she had been afraid for no reason at all, maybe Agatha loved her, really loved her, maybe she didn’t have to pretend anymore.

She was so very bad at pretending.

“Agatha,” she said shakily, her hands massaging the back of Agatha’s neck, in small circles that she hoped communicated the words she was afraid of saying. “I lo—”

And then, for the second time that day, Agatha said, her voice wrecked with terror, “Don’t.” She shook her head, pleading. “Please. Don’t.”

Chapter 23

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

“I — wait, don’t what? ”

The confession died in Rio’s throat. She felt like all the wind had been knocked from her chest. Like an ice bucket had been poured over her head. 

She had been so ready to say everything, it was all bursting out of her like a waterfall, but now…

She glanced at Agatha’s face. At the utter fear set into her brows.

All Rio felt now was worry. Worry because Agatha was looking at her in utter desperation. 

Her fingers were trembling, her eyes blinking like a woman standing in front of a car accident, watching the engine burst into flames.

Rio hadn’t even got the full word out, and Agatha was already shutting down.

Fuck. Fuck. Fuck.

She doesn’t want me.

She’s terrified of me.

I miscalculated.

I – 

No.

Stop running away from your own emotions. You can’t put the cat back inside the bag. It’ll claw its way back out eventually.

“Agatha, please,” she said quietly, “You didn’t let me finish.” She frowned when Agatha just grew more panicked. “Agatha, I promise it’s not — it’s not bad. It’s okay. We don’t have to talk about it now. We can talk about it later, once the funeral is over. I didn’t mean to frighten you.”

That sounded good to Rio’s ears, she thought. Placating. Calming. Even if Agatha didn’t want her, even if she’d read every signal wrong, it didn’t have to be some explosive affair like this. They didn’t have to ruin all their well-laid plans with the nuke that was Rio’s bad timing.

“That’s not it, Rio,” Agatha responded. “We can’t talk about it at all.”

Rio blinked at her.  

And because there really was no better way to encapsulate it — “What?”

“I — the thing you want to say. What you want to discuss. I know what it is. We can’t. We can’t discuss it.” Agatha was repeating the idea like a mantra, biting down on her lip and tapping her foot on the damp grass. “We can’t — I shouldn’t have taken it this far. I got too greedy. I should have known better. Fuck. I always do this to myself. Over and over again.”

Agatha had started talking to herself as if Rio wasn’t there; as if Rio had faded to the background, and Agatha had entered a different dimension entirely. Rio knew Agatha was evasive, she knew that she was defensive, that she had walls, but this was a step beyond. Refusing to acknowledge something entirely? It wasn’t just aggravating, it was maddening. 

And Rio was starting to get, well, mad.

Agatha was shaking her head at herself, refusing to look Rio in the eye as she stared back at the street. Black cars were still rumbling toward the gates.

“Fuck, fuck, fuck,” she muttered. “And at a billionaire’s funeral of all places. Great. Phenomenal.” 

Now Rio was more than mad. She was having that wasp sting of a feeling when someone’s character suddenly started separating from the ideal you had of them in your head, peeling away like chipped paint.

“I’m sorry my feelings are such an inconvenience to you,” she said icily.

Agatha startled like a deer at that, looking at her with wide eyes. As if she’d just remembered Rio was, in fact, still there. Still a living, breathing person.

“You’re never an inconvenience to me,” Agatha replied in a small voice. “Nothing about you ever could be.”

And it sounded so sweet, so genuine, it gave Rio whiplash.

What the fuck was going on?

Agatha continued, shaking her head, “But we can’t do this. We can’t talk about it. I know that must be difficult to hear. To grasp. It’s difficult as fuck for me, too. But we — we can’t discuss it. We can either continue as we were before,” Agatha said, and she looked like every word was adding another pound of stress to her shoulders. “Or we have to stop. Right now. Before…”

“Agatha,” Rio cut in, and her voice was loud, louder than she meant it; she only lowered it when they began to get looks again — women in black dresses, men in sharp, judgemental tuxedos. “What in the world are you talking about? If you’re worried about this changing us irrevocably, then… that’s okay. That’s understandable. But it won’t. I know it won’t. Because I’ve had plenty of time to freak out about that possibility. And I – I know in my heart that there’s no topic we could possibly discuss that would change things for us. Even my, especially my… feelings.”

 She inhaled sharply, feeling her eyes start to redden. She felt like a teenager, like a humiliated adolescent, begging in front of her crush. 

She continued, “Even if you don’t feel the same way. That’s fine. I’ll get over it. But you can’t just expect me to keep it all inside, trapped in my skull, spinning and spinning—”

“Rio,” she snapped. “Stop it.”

Agatha’s eyes, wet with tears, blazed red. Redder than Rio had ever seen them.

And Rio’s hands suddenly went clammy.

“Shit,” Agatha cursed. She looked like she’d put her car in reverse instead of drive, and petered right into someone’s brand new cadillac. “I’m sorry. I didn’t mean to… I can’t control…”

Then she shook her head, cutting herself off.

“Clean your mess up, Agatha,” Agatha said to herself, a quiet admonishment as her eyes blazed red again. She began to massage her own arms. “Clean your mess up. Focus. Focus.”

Agatha’s words sounded tinny and far-away to Rio’s ears. Her vision had begun to go dizzy. And where there was once one Agatha, there were now two. Two frayed copies of a woman in distress, grabbing her softly by the shoulders. Rio’s world felt like it had gone off-axis. 

And in that haze, she began to feel a strange sensation in the back of her skull — a warmth, like water bubbling in from a hot whirlpool. It felt like a drug had been injected into her veins, smooth and soft. Like something was dulling a pain that she could no longer feel.

She felt good. She smiled dopily. She couldn’t remember why she’d been so scared a moment before. Why would she be scared? Agatha was here. 

Nothing had ever been scary since Rio had met Agatha.

And then, to make matters even better, Agatha was cupping her cheek, and smiling at her with the saddest, sweetest smile in the world. 

Rio felt so happy seeing it. She felt so adored, to be looked at like that.

“Rio?” she said softly. “Can you hear me?”

Rio blinked. “Yes?” She could hear nothing else but her, loud and clear.

“Good,” Agatha said, swallowing thickly. “That’s good. Listen closely, okay?”

Rio nodded.

“You are not in love with me.”

Rio felt a sensation in her mind like a tiny paper cut. As if a small knife was scraping against the great fuzzy feeling that was lifting her up like a cloud. She opened her mouth to say something, to protest, although she was not sure about what, but Agatha cut her off with a nod.

“I know it might feel like that, but you’re not. You’re confused,” Agatha’s words were tight; they almost sounded pained — she sighed. “What you’re feeling is just friendship. We’re just friends. We’ve always been friends. For six hundred years. Long time, hm?”

 Rio’s eyebrows furrowed, and Agatha began to talk like she was reading a script she’d read a hundred times before. “Nothing has ever been romantic between us. Sometimes we’re attracted to each other, physically, but that’s it. Nothing else. We’re just friends. Do you understand?”

Rio felt a tide wash over her, then away.

She nodded slowly. “Okay.” She frowned. “I’m sorry. I must have been confused.”

Agatha blinked back tears, and shook her head. “Don’t apologize. Never apologize.” She stared at Rio for a long moment, taking a shuddering breath. Something in her expression seemed to move, as if she was setting another version of herself aside. Rio thought it looked strange, like watching an actor disrobe offstage. 

Agatha cleared her throat. “Do you remember why we’re here?”

Rio narrowed her eyes. Of course she did. Was Agatha messing with her?

“Um, to secure ourselves a new billionaire bloodbag?”

Whatever was haunting Agatha seemed to blink away with her answer. She laughed, and clapped Rio on the back, turning toward the procession, where the funeral director had begun to tap a microphone and summon the crowds to the main courtyard.

“Indeed,” she said breathily. Rio saw her wipe something from under her eye. “Indeed.”

 

***

 

Throughout the rest of the funeral, Rio felt very strange. Not that she felt particularly normal most of the time, but something was decidedly off. The scare she’d had with Wanda’s magic was probably to blame, but Agatha was also acting cagey, so that didn’t really help. 

It was hard to explain. She just kept getting these odd blips of deep… Sadness? Uncertainty? 

It was like — the deacon would be speaking, babbling on about what a great man Tommy was, how he volunteered at the soup kitchen on Saturdays (a lie, she had watched Billy scoff every time he saw that pop up in his calendar), how he cared about each and every one of his employees (he didn’t know Rio’s name well enough to scream the right words while she was killing him), and then out of nowhere she’d feel this pain in the back of her skull:

Not quite physical, not quite mental.

It feels like I left the stove on but every time I go to check, it’s off.

Rio nudged Agatha with her elbow. “I think I’m getting a migraine.”

Agatha didn’t look back at her when she said, strangely somber, “I’m sorry. It’ll pass.”

Rio narrowed her eyes at her.

“Thanks for your condolences, weirdo. Do you have any ibuprofen?”

Agatha laughed coldly. “No. But I’m pretty sure every other person here has coke.”

“Cocaine at a funeral?”

“It’s like a cigarette during a lunch break for rich people.”

Rio hummed. Agatha seemed to be in a dour mood, and Rio wanted so badly to take her out of it. So she tried, “I guess we’ll have to get used to it. Seeing as we’ll be rich people soon.”

Agatha snorted, but it sounded half-hearted. She was obviously distracted. Maybe she was actually listening to the deacon — unlikely. You’d have to wrap Agatha in a straight jacket to get her to listen to a priest. Religion had never been kind to her, nor Rio. It didn’t tend to smile on…

Rio frowned.

Smile on what?

Her skull pulsed again.

Smile on what?

Vampires? That didn’t feel right. It was like her brain had surgically removed the rest of the sentence. All that was left was a gaping hole.

“Agatha,” she whispered quickly, to the chagrin of the people next to them. “What does religion tend to… look down upon?”

Agatha finally turned to her then — obviously so bewildered by Rio’s question that she couldn’t help it — and frowned at her. “Um, I don’t know. Everything?”

Agatha’s eyes were so pretty. Green like the sky. Rio noted to herself to tell Agatha that sometime.  “More specific, please.”

“Well, let me see. Children out of wedlock?”

Rio licked her lips. “Not the one I’m looking for.”

“Lying, theft, greed, excess, gluttony — just about anything fun?”

A woman cleared her throat behind them. Rio glanced sideways at her with blazing red eyes, and she quickly went quiet.

“Doesn’t feel right,” Rio mumbled. “Feels more carnal than that.”

Agatha stilled, and the back of her neck went faintly red.

“Well, religion isn’t very fond of homosexuals.”

Rio’s eyes widened. She felt something crack in the back of her mind. Like a rip in a tent. Only what blew in wasn’t air — but a feeling. That’s it. That’s it. That’s it.

She could see the faint silhouette of a memory in her head, crystallizing then. Only, as it was about to take shape, Agatha’s hand wrapped around her wrist, and she tugged at it urgently.

“I see Billy,” Agatha breathed. “Go time.”

1494

 

It was nearly the turn of the century, and for the first time in her life, Agatha Harkness did not feel like she was the mud being run over by the heavy carriage of time — she felt like the spoke that was turning the wheel. She felt like the driver, the horses, and the whips.

Which is to say she felt free. She felt in charge. She felt important. Evanora Harkness was dead, and Rio Vidal killed her. Rio Vidal killed her, and since then they had been glued to the hip, swerving through the middle ages on black steeds, trotting from town to town in search of good food and good trouble, and Agatha had begun to nurse a bit of a crush.

Nay, worse than that.

Agatha was terribly and torturously in love.

And because Agatha was nothing if not direct, she planned on solving this issue in one simple conversation — a conversation which occurred in a tavern somewhere in the middle of Europe, under the dark cape of night. 

The alcohol was hot, the lights were dim, the bar stools were uncomfortable and prickly, and Rio was sitting across from her looking like a fallen angel fresh out of Heaven’s gates.

“Rio,” Agatha breathed, her breath smelling of cranberries and sharp liquor. She propped her chin on her hand, and stared openly at the other woman. “I have a question to ask you.”

Rio set down her drink and smiled lazily. “Is it if I will get us another round? Because you must know I’ve already run out of money. That nobleman’s pockets were unfortunately light.”

“No,” Agatha chuckled. “Not that.”

“Then what, hm?”

“Do you entertain suitors?”

Rio blinked at her. She looked caught off guard, but not offended. She had that expression on her face that she got when Agatha was trying to explain an elaborate social rule to her.

“Entertain… suitors?”

“I mean to ask if you court. Date. However you’d like to put it.” Agatha tried to make it seem casual by shrugging, but the burn in her cheeks probably ruined her appearance of apathy. “We’ve been traveling for some time now, but I’ve never seen you… take a lover.”

Agatha had taken one or two or three, here and there. But she had always done it in secret, away from Rio. Agatha’s mother had always insisted that women like her — who preferred the fairer sex — did not exist, so she felt a need to prove her incorrect. And incorrect she had proved her, several times over, to the tune of many happy, loud women in various inns and farmhouses.

But Rio had never shown an inclination toward, well, anyone. Even when Agatha would test the waters — remark about a beautiful woman, or (reluctantly) about a handsome man — Rio would just shrug and reply something to the effect of, “If you say so,” and then they would move on with their day. Which both made Agatha feel better and worse.

“I had a lover, once. If you can even call it that,” Rio said, blushing as she took a long sip of her mead. “A boy in my town. In Galicia. He stole a kiss from me. It was not long. Quite wet. Slobbery, like a dog. I would have rather kissed a dog, in all honesty. I pushed him into the river. It did not inspire an interest in romance for me.”

Agatha laughed at the image of Rio pushing a slobbering boy into a stream. “Really? No interest at all, in hundreds of years? I assure you there are better experiences than that out there.”

Rio’s eyebrows rose. “You can assure me? Have you entertained suitors, Agatha?”

Agatha went beet red. Damn it. This woman, and her keen ear.

“Perhaps,” she said. “Although none of consequence.”

Rio seemed suddenly much more interested in this discussion. She leaned forward conspiratorially. “Tell me about the inconsequential ones, then.”

Agatha’s heart beat rabidly in her chest. Rio’s big brown eyes were looking at her with such warmth, such interest, Agatha could imagine for a mere second that she actually wanted to become one of the names on Agatha’s list. That she wanted to become the name. 

The person of consequence.

Stop dreaming, you scoundrel.

Agatha pursed her lips, then said brusquely, “I fear you might judge me and cast me aside.”

Rio looked taken aback, startled. “Cast you aside?”

Agatha shrugged, looking askance. On the stage there was a band playing, a little man with a string instrument, and a handsome woman with a percussive drum. They played loud enough that Agatha could drown out the worries that were spinning around her head. But she could not drown out Rio, who had grabbed her wrist, and pulled it lightly.

“I would not cast you aside, Agatha,” she said quietly.

And because Agatha was drunk, she replied, “Even if I were to steal a kiss from you?”

Rio’s pupils went as wide as saucers.

“Do you intend to?” she whispered, as if something fascinating was happening in front of her.

Agatha’s heart clenched. 

“Steal one? No.” But I’d take one if it came willingly.

“Hm,” was all Rio said, blinking several times. After a moment, she squeezed Agatha’s hand. “So, what is it? You were going to tell me about your affairs. ”

Agatha deflated. For a flicker of a moment she had fooled herself again.

But Rio had said it herself. She was uninterested in romance, and Agatha would have to respect that. But that didn’t mean she couldn’t be honest with her. They were still friends after all.

“Well,” Agatha clicked her tongue, “As long as you promise not to tell the church…”

She looked back and forth conspiratorially — half an act, half serious — before lowering her voice, and looking at Rio with a sly grin.

“If you must know,” she said. “I have come to enjoy the company of women.”

*

 

1544

 

They were riding on horses away from a very mysterious fire — one that happened to kill seventeen wealthy noblemen and twelve wealthy noblewomen — when Rio said to her, as if only one month had passed, and not sixty years, “I have been thinking about your question.”

“What question?” Agatha replied. 

She was disoriented, her pockets full of valuables they had pocketed from the raid.  

“About suitors. About my… proclivities. Romantically.”

Agatha nearly stumbled off her steed.

In sixty years they had not addressed that night once. Well, not explicitly. Agatha had gotten more comfortable taking women into bed in their shared quarters, and Rio had begun to make comments about them. Innocent comments, niceties. But nothing more than that.

“I think I might, too, enjoy the company of women,” Rio said, casually, as if it was nothing.

Agatha’s jaw went slack. She was happy that it was night, and the only thing illuminating their way was the torch Rio was holding, because if Rio could see her clearly, the entire jig would be up then and there. The confession was written on her face.

And then, just as fast, pure jealousy piled into her gut.

“Oh?” Agatha huffed. “And that realization occurred to you when? Before, after, or during our little killing spree?” Had there been a woman there she’d found charming?

“None of the above.” Rio paused. “You sound upset.”

“I am not upset. I am merely… bewildered by your timing.”

“I did not know there was a good time to realize these things.”

Agatha reeled back her misplaced fury, and sighed. “No, there is not. I’m sorry. Let’s start this again. You want to sleep with a woman?”

Even in the dark Agatha could see just how red that comment made Rio.

“Sleep with… I am not sure. I merely… I do not think I like men.”

That, at least, makes Agatha laugh. “I always knew you were a woman of fine taste.”

Rio smiled at her. They came upon a hunter’s shack soon enough, dealt with the hunter occupying it, and settled in for the night, raiding his pantry for alcohol and grains.

Unfortunately the hunter’s bed was quite small, only big enough for one and a half bodies, so they were pressed together that night, forced into a proximity that made Agatha delirious.

They were at the edge of sleep, looking at each other, illuminated only by the moonlight, discussing the events of the day, when Agatha’s deliriousness caught up with her. “I think,” she said, emboldened by the drink and Rio’s confession. “You should kiss me. To see if you’d like it.”

Rio’s eyes went wide, and Agatha immediately felt a surge of panic in her gut.

“To see if you’d like… being with a woman,” she corrected. Then Rio’s eyes went larger, and Agatha realized her error. “I meant only a kiss. Chaste. Nothing more. I won’t — I wouldn’t try anything beyond that of course. I just thought it could be helpful. In your… exploration.”

And to Agatha’s great dismay, Rio did not just nod her head yes, or even say an acceptable, but crushing, No, thank you. She instead laughed, and gave Agatha a sly smile, repeating her sentiment from years ago, “Are you aiming to steal a kiss from me, Agatha Harkness?”

“No,” Agatha breathed, her heart clenching. “Not steal.”

I want you to want it. I need you to want it.

Her thoughts must have shown on her face, because Rio’s smile slowly faltered at Agatha’s earnestness. Her face was overtaken with a foreign expression that Agatha had never seen before. 

One that looked truly startled — scared. Frightened.

And Agatha promptly panicked.

Oh god.

I’ve frightened her.

Agatha’s throat tightened. Her chest felt heavy, breathless.

She’s going to leave me.

She’s going to run and leave me and despise me.

I’m going to be alone again.

Rio opened her mouth to say something, but before she could, Agatha’s eyes burned bright red, and she did something she swore she’d never do to Rio, not on purpose. Something she knew once she began to do, she would never really be able to undo — a pattern which could turn Rio into that thrall in Evanora’s basement, the one she’d been trying to forget for centuries afterward.

But because she was selfish and in love and afraid, Agatha did it anyway, and told her it would just be this one time. Just once, then never again.

“You are asleep,” Agatha said in a rush of words, pressing her hand to Rio’s forehead. “You are having a very strange dream about me. One that you won’t recollect in the morning.”

Rio’s eyelids began to get heavy. Agatha blew out a warm, unsteady breath of relief.

It’s working. It’s working.

Rio slowly fell into the caress of sleep, and Agatha watched her for half the night afterwards, wrapped up in a whirlpool of guilt and rejection. A deadly cocktail that only waded away the next morning, when she awoke to Rio’s arm wrapped around her middle, and the other girl smiling this strange little grin, nudging her nose into her cheek.

“I had the most wonderful dream,” she hummed. “You were in it.”

“Oh?” Agatha put on her mask, and schooled her face. It’s not what you want it to be. A woman has hundreds of dreams in a night.  “Tell me about it?”

Rio laughed into her neck. Her breath was as cold as the air outside.

“I can’t remember what happened, exactly,” she said. “Only that it ended too soon.”
