Chapter 24

Chapter Notes

Surprise! Two chapters in one day. Because I hate angst as much as you guys do (ok, well, I also love it, but), and I wanted to give my girl Agatha a chance to... start explaining herself. Now I will take a break for the day and go back in my hole. Love yall.

As Agatha dragged her toward Billy, Rio’s mind felt like an open wound.

Flits of memory were seeping out like bloodstains. 

They were so brief, like one-second films. Agatha’s face over the centuries — smiling, teeth bared, laughing, full-bodied, head thrown back. Agatha in the dim light of a pub, in the broad light of day, blood on her face, murder on her hands. 

Why are you showing me this now, she asked to her brain, seeing no red thread between the memories. The only thread was Agatha.

“I think Wanda’s magic messed with me,” Rio confessed.

Agatha’s grip on her hand tightened but she did not look back. They threaded through men and women, shouldering through the crowd, getting nasty looks from every direction.

“How so?” Agatha said quietly.

Rio could see Billy’s broccoli-like curls now. He was standing on the outskirts of the service, leaning on the stone fencing, smoking a cigarette. It looked like it might as well have been his first cigarette ever from the way his mouth puckered like he’d just tasted acid.

“It’s like someone keeps rapidly changing the channel in my brain. I keep visualizing things I don’t mean to visualize.” As she said the words aloud, Rio frowned. “I think this has happened before but I can’t place it. Have we run into the Maximoffs before this?”

Agatha didn’t reply, dragging her even more quickly towards the boy. They skidded to a stop in front of him, and his eyes widened. He panicked, throwing the cigarette to the ground and stomping on it with his dress shoe. A dress shoe that was also too large for him, slipping at the heel. His entire ensemble was like that — an unfitting tan coat, too-tight dress pants.

 Agatha rolled her eyes. “Jesus, kid. We’re not your parents.” She eyed his jacket pocket, which was bulging with the cigarette container. “Having a vice actually makes you almost likeable. Dampens the whole obsessive dorky thing. Now hand one over, pretty please.”

He mumbled, “You’re not supposed to smoke here.”

Agatha rolled her eyes and ignored his hypocrisy, digging the cigarette packet out of his pocket, plucking a Lucky Strike out and then making a gimme gesture with her hands for a lighter. He gave her a scandalized expression before finally handing it over. She lit it quickly, tapping her foot anxiously as the smoke clouded. Rio gave her a bewildered look.

She’s nervous. Why is she so nervous?

“We need to talk to your step-dad,” Agatha said, sans all her usual evasiveness. She usually preferred to disorient her conversational partner into giving her what she wanted. This time, it was straight to the point, no lubrication. It was almost concerning. “We’ve talked to a bunch of funeral guests and it’s looking more and more like he’s our guy. So we need to get some one-on-one time with him. Think you can get us past those guards?”

Agatha gestured toward the flank of human-beef in tuxedos that was standing nondescriptly at the far north of the reception, beyond the deacon and the body. 

Billy looked suddenly incredibly nervous. Which was saying something, because he always looked incredibly nervous. “You really think it was Richard who did it?”

Agatha said something in response, Rio saw her lips move, but she couldn’t hear the words. And Billy must have said something back, because he was giving a somber shake of his head, and beginning to turn on his heel to walk towards them. 

Rio frowned. 

What the hell is going on? 

Why can’t I hear them?

Her brain was — it wasn’t working. 

It felt like a slowly dying machine. She couldn’t hear the present. Like the audio cord had been snipped. Instead it kept showing her pictures of the past, over and over and over and over, like a rapidly flitting film reel. All the same moments, regurgitated, again and again.

Agatha in dark robes at the peak of a mountain, Austrian alps at her back.

Agatha next to her in bed in a cabin. Flush to her body. So close she could feel Agatha’s breath on her cheek, Agatha’s red lips curling into a smile.

Agatha in Bucharest. In the night. They’re running from the guards. Why are we running? They did something bad. They killed a man — no, not just any man. A very significant man. They killed him and now they were running for their lives. They got out of sight, dodged into an alleyway. Agatha looked beautiful pressed up against the wall, her hands stained with blood, her hair wet from the rain. Rio wanted to run her hands through it. She refrained. Agatha stared at her with a strange expression on her face, a deeply thoughtful one.

Then Agatha said something Rio couldn't hear. And then Agatha kissed her.

Back in the present, Rio dropped her cocktail glass. It shattered on the ground.

She kissed me.

Rio searched her mind like a feral hound for what came next. But after that kiss was a blank slate. Rio tried to find the next memory on the docket, but time seemed to skip forward, and suddenly she was in the North. In Denmark. Norway? She was watching the Borealis. They were underwhelming. She was writing letters to Agatha while Agatha was in Romanian prison. 

Agatha was in prison because she was found kissing another woman. 

No, not another woman.

She kissed me.

She kissed me and then I left and she threw herself in prison.

That makes no sense.

Why did I leave?

Why did she kiss me?

Her mind hurt so badly. It was like she’d had a geyser suppressed under a pot lid, and with the lid gone, hot, molten water was spilling out like a fire hose. She squatted to the ground, jaw clenching and unclenching as she massaged her temples.

Then, a booming voice. “William, what is the meaning of this?” 

“Richard, these are my coworkers, Agnes and uh —”

“Your coworkers? Your brother is dead. I am in no mood to socialize.”

“Rio.” It was Agatha’s voice now, high and concerned. “Rio, breathe, breathe. It’s okay.”

A new voice echoed. A woman’s, high and lilting. “Billy, there you are. I was looking all over for — Agatha?”

“Oh, for fuck’s sake.”

“Agatha Harkness?”

“Harkness? Wanda, what kind of filth did you drag into my son’s funeral?”

“Calm down, Richard.”

“Rio, we’re leaving. Rio, deep breaths. Come on.”

Rio had the faint notion that she was being picked up. Hauled into Agatha’s arms and transported on foot through the crowds, pushing noisily past the body, possibly toppling over several flower arrangements. 

Everything was a blur until she heard the sound of a taxi rolling up on the pavement, and there was that other woman’s voice again, following them around like the plague. She still couldn’t see anything, but she could hear what sounded like a budding argument.

“Agatha, we need to talk.”

“Not the time, ginger Dracula. Get the fuck out of my way.”

Wanda seemed to ignore her comments completely. “I heard rumors about the spell you had on Ms. Death Incarnate, but it was hard to believe anyone could have tamed the beast herself. Seeing it with my own eyes I’m still not sure I believe it. Murder herself, a thrall.”

“She’s not a thrall.” Rio could feel the tension in Agatha’s hands as she held her. “Do you want me to put you into the ground right now, or later? Because it’s looking increasingly like right now,” Agatha spat. Rio felt a wave of nausea, and began to groan. Agatha paused, panicking. “Oh god, she’s going green. Oh god. Rio, breathe. Breathe. Fuck. Fuck.”

“Her brain is melting. Did you unwind her?”

“No. She’s — I don’t know what’s happening. But her brain is not melting. I didn’t unwind her. She’s just… recovering.”

“Recovering from your tampering, you mean,” Wanda said. It didn’t sound judgmental, but she did punctuate it with a click of her tongue. “Do you want my help or would you like her to die in the backseat of some taxi? I’d pick quickly, before fate picks for you.”

Nausea consumed her, and Rio threw up onto the pavement. Agatha sucked in a breath.

“Baby, it’s okay. It’s okay.” Agatha began to sound desperate. Rio’s mind spun around baby, baby, baby. “How in the hell are you going to help?”

“I can give you time to unwind her properly.” Rio heard the car’s engine rev. “Consider it a favor that I’ll cash in at a later time.”

“No, no, no. I – I can’t unwind her. It might kill her. Don’t you think I would have done it already if I thought I could do it safely? Do you really think I keep her like this because I want to?” Agatha’s voice broke. “I only did this once – once – on purpose. Every time after has been – has been out of my control. And then it stacks and it stacks. And the more it stacks, the deeper the lies penetrate the mind, the harder it is to – to — I can’t stop it, Wanda. ”

“Agatha.” Wanda’s voice dropped lower. “The shell can’t contain her anymore. You don’t have a choice in the matter. Either you watch her die, or you at least say you tried.”

A long drawn out silence filled the air. Rio’s mind buzzed like a construction site. Black flashes paraded behind her eyes. She felt herself heading for certain destruction. Fast and fiery, like a racecar who’d lost a wheel, and was now dragging its metal body across the pavement.

“Agatha,” Rio mumbled into the air. “Please.”

Agatha swallowed.

“Okay,” she said. “Wanda, do it. ”

 

***

 

Rio woke up in their Airbnb. All the shades were drawn, so it was completely dark.

She could feel a warm compress laid over her forehead. Her entire body was beaded with sweat, sticking to the couch cushions. Her surroundings filtered in slowly, and then all at once.

She took in a sharp breath.

“Fuck,” she breathed. “I’m alive.”

The last thing she remembered was Wanda and Agatha’s conversation at the car. She felt instant, deep-seated relief that she remembered that at all.

Because that meant she also remembered everything else.

Well, everything was incredibly relative — she had begun to understand that there were holes the size of craters in her mind, and they weren’t of her own making.

She had always assumed that it was time that eroded her memory — but now she couldn’t tell the difference between life’s natural degradation and the surgical scars Agatha had inflicted on her over the years. She could hardly believe that was the truth, that Agatha had really cut her mind open and stitched her back, placing falsity after falsity, laying blankets over the wrinkles of her brain. Over and over. For who knows how long. 

Rio expected to feel disgust at what Agatha had made her into. Raw and vitriolic. She expected to feel a rage that filled her chest to the point of flowing over.

But instead she just felt empty as a carcass.

Like her brain had been put in water, and was floating, floating. She got the sensation that if it wasn’t for Wanda’s magic, she’d be somewhere else right now. In the beyond.

But she wasn’t there yet. Instead, she was here, feeling close to that tether — but not quite there. Death’s doorstep, her hand on the knob, but the door remained firmly closed. 

It reminded her of something one of the priests in Galicia said to her. One of the old men in robes that liked to lurk at the bakery and bother her with a life lesson.

She’d thought him silly then, but she saw his point very clearly now. His point about how, at the end of one’s life, we resent people not for what they did to us — but for the lacking nature of their explanations. 

We resent the parent who neglected us without reason, yet we pity, and sometimes even adore, the parent who neglected us while he toiled for money for our food. While he pursued his passions at our expense. It is not the action, it is the reasoning behind it. 

Rio did not fancy herself a psychologist. But she couldn’t resist the notion that what burned in her chest was not anger, but a curiosity — a need to know — as fierce as the sun. She gripped her fingers around the back of the couch, and began to lean forward.

I have to believe she didn’t do this on purpose.

This was Agatha. This was the girl who learned how to Suggest by un -suggesting.

This was the woman who would break thralls out of their stupor because she hated to see someone innocent put under that kind of spell.

She isn’t her mother. She isn’t Evanora.

  “Don’t get up. Please. Just stay on the couch.”

Agatha’s voice gave her a jolt. She sounded incredibly exhausted, more exhausted than Rio had ever heard her, even when they’d been near-death in the woods outside Bucharest.

She disobeyed Agatha’s instructions and leaned upward, cringing when her entire body ached in response. Still, she made it to a seated position just in time for Agatha to round the couch, brows knitted in worry as she set down a tea cup by the table. 

“I told you to stay still.”

“And I don’t know if listening to you is the best idea for me.”

Agatha froze at that, at Rio’s tone.

“Rio, I — I promise I didn't mess with too much. It's only a few memories, a handful, over six hundred years — okay, two handfuls, but —”

Rio shook her head. “Agatha, I don’t want an apology right now. I’m sure I deserve one. Knowing you, I potentially deserve thousands. But you are terrible at them, and I am flirting much too comfortably with death, so please spare me my time on Earth for other, more useful things,” she finished, then grabbed Agatha’s palm, tugging her toward her. 

Agatha, eyes wide, fell to the side of the couch, and just stared, and stared, like a frightened child. As if Rio was the thing that consumed her—and not the other way around. 

And maybe that’s how Agatha saw it. Maybe Rio had been the one terrorizing her , somehow. Maybe Agatha had been protecting herself from something Rio couldn’t understand.

Maybe, maybe, maybe . Maybes were the problem here. 

Rio did not know what words she was going to say until she was saying them, “I want you to tell me everything, from the beginning, right from the moment we met, until now. Everything you suppressed. Every reason why. And then I will decide if I want to kill you, or kiss you.”

Agatha stilled. Blinked. A single tear streaked down from her eye.

“Releasing it all —” Agatha paused. “It might kill you.”

Rio knew very well that could be true. Agatha had told her what happened to that one thrall she released from Evanora’s control. How Agatha had been trying everyday to forget it. Rio knew what could happen when you tampered too much.

And yet. 

“I’d rather die knowing you,” she said, “Than die feeling like I never did.”

Chapter 25

Chapter Summary

Rio remembers.

Rio had always wondered what might it feel like to feel death close at hand. 

She had gotten her closest taste in starvation, back in the weeds outside Bucharest, lying hand in hand with Agatha in the field as they listened to the vampire hunters a few leagues off, crossbows cocked, stakes in hand. Two predators, reduced to prey. 

What had been most surprising to Rio was how sweet the air had tasted then. When your body is reduced to nothing, when you’ve been wrung like a rag, everything — the air, the taste on your tongue, the thoughts on your head — takes on a faint cotton candy feeling. Nothing feels scary anymore. It just feels floaty — it feels funny, like a cosmic joke God is cracking at the end.

She remembered the way Agatha’s hand had tightened around hers then, and how the last thing she’d said, before Rio blacked out, had been a joke. A stupid one, about bees. They both laughed at it like schoolchildren, silly with the delusion that everything would be okay.

Rio remembered feeling her heart swell so big, it was a miracle she survived it.

Now felt different. But it also felt the same.

Because no matter how much she wanted to be angry at Agatha, all she could do was watch her lips, and wait for the joke that it would make it all better again.

“Before we begin, I have to lay a few ground rules,” Agatha huffed, because even in the face of maybe her greatest personal sin, she still had the audacity to be annoyed at logistics. “One, you can’t ask me anything about any of the memories until it’s over. Completely over. And two, you can’t die on me. Graveyard slots in New York City are way out of our budget, and Willy’s money supply has already run as dry as the Sahara.”

And there it was. Agatha’s favorite coping mechanism – making light of an incredibly non-light situation — but Rio didn’t have the energy to engage with it, her lips heavy like dumbbells. Believe her, she tried; she got halfway to opening her mouth and saying His name is Billy, and the reason that money is gone is because you spent it on shoes, when she got another flash of black, and she winced, pressing her hand to forehead. Agatha immediately grabbed her shoulder.

“Fuck, okay, okay, fuck,” Agatha repeated out loud, apparently trying to center herself by using as many expletives as humanely possible. She began to mutter to herself again. “Don’t kill her because you can’t keep your big fucking mouth shut for two seconds. Concentrează-te.”

Agatha squeezed her eyes shut, and blew out a slow breath.

When her eyes snapped open again, they were burning bright, lava red. Lava being the apt word — because a red current was swirling around her pupils, clockwise. Like a serpent.

So this is what unwinding looks like, Rio thought.

The process of un-thralling the thralled.

Peeling back duct-tape wrapped around the subconsciousness.

She’d only seen Agatha do it a handful of times, all within a week of their first meeting. Once Evanora was dead, Agatha insisted on freeing all her long-time servants — all except for one.

The doorman. 

Rio had asked why. It seemed a strange decision, given that Agatha had been especially close to him. She had insisted that he and their late maid, Elisabeth, had raised her more than her own mother had. But back then, Agatha had refused to meet her eyes when she answered the question, “He’s buried too deep.”  

Agatha had instead left him the keys to the castle, given him a kiss to the cheek, and a simple final instruction — Live, or at least try to.

As Agatha’s gaze latched onto her, Rio wondered if she was the same. 

Buried too deep.

Agatha didn’t give her long to contemplate it, snapping her fingers in front of Rio’s face to grab her attention. Rio startled. She hadn’t realized she’d faded out, gone somewhere else. From the worried look on Agatha’s face, that somewhere probably looked a little too much like death.

“Listen up. When I begin, you will experience the memories as if you are living them for the first time. They’ll feel like dreams, but more visceral,” she instructed. When Rio arched her eyebrow, as if to say, how do you know, Agatha muttered, “I tried it on myself once.”

“You did? When?”

Agatha shook her head. “We can talk about this after, Rio.”

“I’d like to know now,” Rio breathed, realizing with a quiet rush of urgency that she wasn’t ready, “Actually, not only that. I’d like to ask a few questions before you open me up.” 

“Rio, we don’t have —” Agatha raised her voice, then paused, realizing, most likely, that she did not want to spend her last moments with Rio yelling at her, even if it was tempting, “Wanda’s magic isn’t infinite. She’s distorting the space inside of your brain to slow down the deterioration. But I’m not sure by how much. Or for how long. So time is, well, of the essence.”

“And yet you seem very comfortable wasting it by arguing with me.”

Agatha blinked at her, utterly speechless, enough to just shake her head, then make a defeated fine gesture with her hands.

“Even in death you’re insufferable. Fine, out with it, quickly.”

“Answer my first question,” Rio said adamantly, refusing to let her own indecision show.

Because she knew Agatha was right. That she was wasting time. But the truth was she’d never been in a position before where she knew that she could ask Agatha something, and she’d answer with the truth. All of the truth. The woman was a closed book with a lock and a picket fence.

Agatha sucked in a breath, and looked toward the fridge as she said, “Fine. I – When I used Suggestion on myself, I was trying to forget the memory of what I did to Elisabeth. It didn’t work. Big surprise. You can’t suppress your own shit. I would have been better off ramming my head repeatedly against the wall or letting Evanora give me a concussion. Next.”

Rio’s gut twisted. Even though a part of her was mad at Agatha beyond belief, betrayed and tender, she still couldn’t resist saying, “You didn’t do anything to Elisabeth. It’s not your fault that Evanora suppressed her so deeply that she couldn’t handle coming out of that shell.”

Agatha laughed coldly, and Rio could tell her attempt at comfort had slid around Agatha’s body like oil to water. “Your unending empathy is as flattering as it is concerning, but it was my fault. It was my decision. I could have just left her alone. She seemed happy enough as she was. But I couldn’t resist. I wanted to make her whole. It was selfish. All because I can never keep my stupid, insatiable fingers off of something, even if it means ruining it completely.”

“Is that how you feel about me? Like I’m something you’re scared to ruin?”

Agatha’s face faltered.

“I —” She frowned. Rio could see a hundred different masks threaten to shove their way into her expression. In any other situation they would. But in this one, Agatha just breathed, “Yes.”

Rio laughed, and the movement in her chest caused her whole body to shudder in pain, but she still managed out, “And you call me insufferable. You’ll kill an innocent man for an Amazon gift card, but in other regards you hold yourself to the moral standards of the Pope.”

Agatha huffed. “Sue me for considering you more important than a man.”

“What do you consider me, Agatha?”

Agatha frowned at the question, and picked up the tea she’d set aside for Rio. “This is getting cold. You should drink it before it’s freezing —”

“Agatha.”

“Rio.” Agatha inhaled sharply, desperately, meeting her eyes with a fierceness. “We’re being reckless. Opening the wound ahead of the unwinding… It’s a bad idea. You need to be in the right state of mind. And you’re already deteriorating.”

“For the love of God, Agatha, stop trying to protect me!” Rio snapped. “I could die in a minute. Just tell me. Let me in.”

Agatha’s jaw tightened.

“I’ll answer any other question.”

“No. That’s my only one.”

Agatha’s foot had begun tapping erratically on the carpet. “You’re going to die if we don’t hurry this along, Rio. Are you suicidal?”

“Not yet, but I’ll get there if you don’t give me a straight answer for once in your goddamn life.”

Agatha’s hands were shaking, trembling. 

“Fine, then, I consider you a royal, aching , pain in my ass —”

“Agatha. ”

And then, like a shaken bottle exploding from the cap, Agatha screamed, covering her head with her hands, dragging her fingers along her cheeks. The tea mug collapsed onto the rug, green liquid spilling like a tide. Rio’s mouth fell open in surprise. Because Agatha had completely lost her cool — had abandoned it on the side of the highway, left to fester.

Rio had never seen her look so reckless. Her facial expressions so rabid and unschooled, her fingers trembling.

Then, a moment later, Agatha’s shoulders slumped. Her hands slowly descended down her face, revealing her jaw, clenched back in place, her eyes hollow. The only hint that she was still undergoing anything at all was the way her neck bobbed—the way her voice was thick and salty.

“I’m sorry,” Agatha said coldly, then inched forward on the couch. Her hand wrapped around Rio’s leg, then slunk up to her thigh, bracketed her hips, and suddenly Agatha was on top of her, knees on either side of Rio’s thighs, head looming over Rio’s like a gargoyle. “But I’m going to take away your agency, one last time, because you talk too much.”

She threaded her hands through Rio’s hair, keeping her skull in place, then looked deeply into her eyes. And for a moment a bit of tenderness seeped through — a look that said I can’t promise you much, but I’ll promise I’ll miss you if I lose you — and then before Rio could say anything to that, Agatha’s red eyes spun like a hurricane, and Rio was pulled into the eye of the storm.

 

***

 

The first time it happened, they were in a hunter’s cabin. Rio didn’t recall this memory at all. It had to have been the deepest, most buried seed. The root of the tree.

It was night, and it was cold, and their bodies were pressed close like two dogs huddled for warmth, and Agatha told her to kiss her. Rio’s heart had beaten so fast in reply that she didn’t know what to do with it. She didn’t understand why it was trying to leap out of her chest. 

It’s just a kiss. She’s just being nice. She’s just being Agatha.

Rio had stared at her friend’s pretty face and knew immediately that she did want to kiss her. It wasn’t a question of want, it was a question of how much and how long . 

Because she got the uncanny feeling in her chest that her desire would immediately outweigh Agatha’s, that the moment Rio touched her lips to Agatha’s, she’d be desperate up to the very second that Agatha withdrew, and she’d miss it for every moment afterwards.

So she said something stupid. Something like, “Are you aiming to steal a kiss from me, Agatha Harkness?” And Agatha had obviously hated that — for a reason Rio couldn’t understand, Agatha had hated it so much that she pressed her hand to Rio’s mind and trapped it in a dream.

 

***

 

The second time, Rio remembered the faint outline of the memory. 

They were traveling in the early 1800s, on one of the first passenger locomotives. The train was so noisy Rio had stuffed cotton in her ears, and Agatha was fiercely journaling across from her, one leg hoisted up to the seat, a notebook pressed against it, a pen in the other.

She looks stupidly lovely, was what Rio thought at the time, and also now. She was younger, before the first age lines had settled in, wearing a long black dress, leather boots, and a tan winter jacket that draped lazily around the seat, deterring other passengers. It was one of those fancy ones only the rich could afford. Agatha had stolen it off some Swedish princess.

“What are you writing?” Rio asked.

“A book,” Agatha said, not looking up. Then, she paused, in thought. “No, a manifesto. That’s a better name. More arresting. I want something that will get people out of their seats.”

Rio chuckled. “I thought books were for getting people in seats.”

“Not this one.” Agatha smirked, then raised her head, turning the notebook around so Rio could see. The original title was A Book On Common-Ownership , but book had been crossed out, and the title rearranged underneath, so now it was named The Common-Ownership Manifesto. “I’m conducting an experiment. A bit more ambitious than my usual ones.”

She tapped the pen to her chin, and it dabbed a blip of ink there. Rio stared at it as Agatha continued, “I thought it’d be interesting to see if I could change the societal order of things around by suggesting a minimal number of people. Just slip this into the pockets of a few prominent leaders and see what might happen. We’re heading to France soon, so maybe I’ll start there.”

She shrugged, then tossed the notebook onto the table. Rio’s eyes slid down to the page, where a few other ideas had been crossed out before this one. There was one called See the Maximoffs about the California Gold Rush. Rio squinted at it.

“The Maximoffs,” she breathed. “Who are they?”

Agatha had her hand raised, flitting it around obnoxiously to try and wave down one of the staff members. “Old enemies of Evanora. The wife Wanda is an on-and-off pen pal of mine,” Agatha said simply. “Last I heard they were getting friendly with President Polk. It seems there’s going to be a lot of shiny minerals flowing into that most western state of theirs, and since we’re hit a bit hard as far as our financials, I thought, maybe…”

Agatha made a noncommittal gesture with her hands, and then brightened when an attendant finally noticed her aggressive waving. 

“Finally. I’ve been trying to get your attention for hours, I was scared my shoulder would soon sustain a permanent injury,” Agatha huffed. Rio had been counting — the other woman had been waiting approximately fifteen seconds. “Two coffees, please. Don’t go light on the sugar. And three pastries. Any kind. Preferably with jam.”

The attendant nodded with an apology, and scurried off. A minute later he was back with their food, Agatha paid him with a dizzying bat of her eyelashes, and soon enough they were tucking into it ravenously like a pack of wolves. It’d been many hours since they’d last eaten, the trains were horribly slow and made a hundred stops heading southward, and by the time they were done, both their faces were covered with a dusting of powdered sugar.

“You’ve got —” Rio gestured with an amused smile to Agatha’s face, which was now the victim of both pen ink and various schmutz, “Here, let me.”

She pushed their empty dishes to the side and leaned over the table. The train rocked at the exact moment she meant to steady herself, and she fell forward, knocking her forehead against Agatha’s. It was a hard hit — enough to make them both wince — and dizzying enough that Rio accidentally brushed her lips up against Agatha’s when she meant to withdraw.

The sound Agatha made was something between a startle and a whimper at the contact, and Rio’s eyebrows flung upward, blinking quickly. “Oh – I’m sorry.”

When she withdrew, Agatha was staring at her, cheeks flushed, and eyes red as saucers.

And not just red, but swirling.

“Agatha?” Rio said, brow furrowing. “Are you—”

In the memory, Rio felt herself freeze. But since she was reliving it now, simply a passenger, an external viewer, she could see the aftermath. Her body slumped to the side, and she fell asleep. Another memory subjugated into a dream. Meanwhile, Agatha’s hands whisked up to her face, looking completely shocked.

“Rio— I didn’t mean to—” she mumbled. “Oh god, what did I do?”

When Rio awoke, hours later, Agatha had a vacant look in her eye. Back then, she didn’t know why. Now she was beginning to.

 

***

 

The next time she kissed Agatha, it wasn’t a mistake.

It was soon after, maybe only a decade, and even though Rio’s mind didn’t remember their accidental kiss, it was almost like her body did. They were visiting Riegersburg for the first time in three hundred years to fetch an old heirloom of Agatha’s. 

They were tight on funds (some things never change) and Agatha insisted one of Evanora’s old scrolls could sell for a pretty penny on the vampire black market. Rio didn’t even know there was a vampire black market, but then again Rio didn’t care to know many things about vampires — she left the socializing to Agatha.

Rio had brought her daguerreotype camera on the trip, excited to capture the plunging valleys and rising mountains that she remembered seeing from Riegersburg. The view from the castle after she’d killed the Harkness clan had been one of her favorite memories. 

Not for the killing, even though the revenge was truly sweet, but because it was her very first memory with Agatha—the other vampire holding her blood-stained hand as they looked out over the landscape, and knew that this Earth belonged to them now. 

That memory was like an aphrodisiac, one that made Rio feel giddy and stupid as she followed Agatha around the courtyard, snapping pictures as Agatha made small talk with the castle’s current custodians. 

They weren’t thralls — Agatha didn’t keep thralls — but they were as close as you could get, which meant they were well-paid employees. Agatha gave them salaries higher than twice the median Austrian average, which meant they didn’t question where the money came from, or why their boss seemed to never age through the decades.

“Here it is,” Agatha said with a breath of relief. They were in the attic, moonlight seeping through as Agatha finally pried the top off an old chest. The claustrophobic space smelled like dust and old wood, and it was oddly comforting. Even more so with Agatha there. 

Rio was busying herself by toying with old daggers —the Harkness’s had their faults, but taste in weaponry was not one of them — when Agatha crawled up to her in the tight space, and proudly displayed the scroll.

Rio hummed, taking it, “So this is that old-fashioned magic you were telling me about?”

The scroll looked like a business contract, only hundreds of years old, and written in runes and hieroglyphs.

“Mhm,” Agatha said, taking it back from her, wrapping it up, and securing it tightly with a string. “Mass Suggestion contracts. Lets you brainwash a whole trove of sorry idiots at once, all you have to do is get them to sign the paper. Mother sparingly used scrolls like these, too much overhead, but she did collect them for rainy days , so to speak. You know, when she wanted to stamp out a peasant movement, or any kind of discussion about civil rights…”

Rio smiled tightly. “Charming woman, Evanora.”

Agatha barked out a laugh. If Rio had been focused on the actual contract at that time, she would have probably burned it then and there. Not let Agatha sell it to the highest bidder.

But like many times in both the past and the future, she was looking at nothing but Agatha. Who, despite the fact that she should be excited that they dug up such a prize, looked nothing but forlorn, distraught. Lost in thought. She was staring out the small box window, looking at the full moon, with her arms wrapped around her legs.

Rio crawled toward her, sidling up to her side and putting her head on Agatha’s shoulder. She could feel the other woman melt against her. It made Rio’s heart beat in that odd way.

“Your mother is gone,” Rio whispered. “She’s not in this room. She’s not in this castle. She’s not on this Earth. Well — that might be a stretch. Morsels of her corpse are under the ground, probably feeding a family of worms. But that’s the limit of her reach, darling. Worms.”

Agatha didn’t move, didn’t respond, for a moment. The only sound was her boot tapping nervously on the floor. Then her hand snaked up Rio’s back, pulling her in closer, so Rio was pressed fully flush to Agatha’s side. Rio could hear Agatha’s blood rush under skin—faster than usual, hectic, her heart had to have picked up pace.

“How did you know?” Agatha replied quietly.

“You’re more readable than you think you are. Did you know you always tap your foot when you’re nervous?”

Agatha laughed quietly. “Thanks for letting me know. I’ll make sure to destroy all my tells.”

“Please don’t,” Rio said, lips twitching up. “I prefer you this way.”

“Sad?”

“Honest.”

Agatha turned to her head to look at her. Her fangs were biting down on her bottom lip, like a dam sitting in front of a waterfall, and her eyes were glassy. Rio could see the reflection of the moon in her eyes. Two full white pearls.

“I’d have to disagree. Honesty doesn’t become me,” Agatha said, brushing under her eye with a wet laugh. “I’m such an ugly crier. Like a smudged painting.”

Rio laughed, disagreeing so completely that she said something rash — one of those thoughts that leaves you before you can think better of it. 

“If you were a painting, I’d be one of those strange men trying to kiss it when the museum guards are looking the other way.”

Agatha’s pupils darkened, and Rio heard her heartbeat staccato.

“You’d do what?”

Rio’s cheeks reddened. “I — well. You’re beautiful, Agatha. That’s all I meant.”

“Is that all you meant?”

Now Agatha was looking at her with a challenge in her eyes. All of that sad apathy had melted away, replaced with this hunger — no, that couldn’t be right. They had eaten plenty that morning, sucked a poor peasant man dry. And yet Agatha was turning toward her, looking at her face, then her neck, at the pale exposed skin, and back again.

“Rio,” Agatha said lowly, quietly, “I must confess something.”

Rio blinked, and felt as if perhaps her life was about to change. “Yes?”

“I dream of biting you almost every night.”

Rio’s mouth went dry, her entire body going rigid. She could barely get out the words when she asked, “Are you starved? We can find another blood source in the nearby village.”

Agatha shook her head slowly back and forth. “No, not like that.” In the darkness she almost looked like a spectre, and Rio wondered for a moment if Evanora really had haunted the place, because this felt like a dream. A dream that was slowly becoming real, undoubtedly real — because Agatha was slipping over her, pressing her down into the wood.

“Agatha…” Rio swallowed slowly. Her entire body was heating up like a furnace. She could feel want, no — need — pulsing from her fingers to her ankles. It rushed into her abruptly, she hadn’t even realized it was there, hibernating, but it had broken through now, full force.

Agatha loomed over her and asked, quietly, “Do you want to kiss me?”

And when she asked it, her eyes were strangely red. A trick of the light.

“What will it mean if I do?” Rio answered.

“It means I’ll make love to you in my mother’s attic, and forever traumatize her ghost.”

And Rio wanted to laugh, she really did, but instead her mind zeroed in on that one word in particular — love — and she trembled. A tremble that traveled throughout her whole body, like a chill in the night, and Agatha must have felt it, because all the confidence, all that hunger, seemed to whisk out of her like the wind, and she began to shake her head.

“I’m — I’m sorry. This was presumptuous, I…”

But Rio wouldn’t let her pull away. Something carnal took her, and she reached upward, hooking her hand behind Agatha’s neck, and pulled her in.

And when their lips touched, it felt like a miracle.

Something blossomed in Rio’s chest. A weed that grew flowers. They didn’t need to take her clothes off for the realization to hit her like an anvil, as if it’d been dormant there all this time.

I love Agatha, she knew already, in the same way that she knew the days of the month. I am desperately in love with Agatha , was a revelation that was new, and terrifying, and all at once made perfect sense. All it took was hearing the other woman to make the sound —

But soon enough Rio was lying slack, again. Asleep. 

And Agatha was alone.

And Rio, the present Rio, stood a few feet away, a passive observer, watching her body lie unmoving on the floor of the attic. She watched as Agatha breathed heavily, crawling off of her in disbelief, backing away, fearful like some kind of terrified animal. She watched Agatha bring her knees to her chest, and hug them tightly, and cry, silently, as the moon descended into the fields.

 

Chapter 26

Unlike the other memories, this one—Agatha, in the attic, in the dim light of the moon dipping below the Alps—did not fade into the next. Rio wouldn’t let her brain skip forward.

 She couldn’t leave Agatha like this. 

Even when she knew it was irrational, even when she knew it was like trying to kiss a photograph, or hold onto a ghost. Because she swore she felt her body there in that attic, really there, feet planted to the wood, fingers trembling. 

She could see the moon through the window and every pockmarked crevice in the basalt walls. She could see Agatha curled into herself like a child on the floor, and Rio wanted to touch her. Needed to touch her. 

To wrap her hands around her small shoulders and tell her, in sequential order—

It’s not your fault. None of this is your fault.

And then,

You’re not alone anymore. We’re in this loop together now.

 Because this was real, it wasn’t a memory. She was there, and she could fix it.

Except the moment she stepped forward, a pain like death pried its fingers into the gummy fabric of her brain. She fired off signal after signal to her useless body—Move. Go to her. She needs you. She needs you.—and yet Rio looked down at her feet and found them as heavy as sandbags, immobile even after she screamed at them like a frenzied football coach.

“Stop it, Rio. You’re making it worse—”

Rio’s heart clenched with hope when she heard the voice, and looked toward the Agatha on the floor expectantly. But that Agatha was still curled up like an armadillo, crying silently. 

The voice had come from somewhere else.

Somewhere else? There was nowhere else. Rio’s mind pulsed as she descended further into the memory, until the word memory was no longer something that could describe what she was feeling. This was all there was anymore. She would stay here until she could fix this.

Rio tried stepping forward again, but the movement seemed to have the opposite effect, and she moved backwards instead of forwards, the image of Agatha getting slowly farther away and more blurry, as if Rio was swimming in a pool, and someone had tied rope around her ankles, and now they were pulling her up like an anchor that’d been tossed to sea. 

“No,” Rio cried out. “Let me go. Let me go to her.”

Her slowly decomposing brain compensated her confusion with a ballista of images. She saw herself falling from a cliff, then suffocating deep underwater. It was too much air and then not enough. She clawed uselessly at the waves, desperate to find something to hold onto. Nothing felt solid anymore. There was nothing to wrap her fingers around.

This is death, she realized, eyes widening. 

And promptly got pissed the fuck off. Because she’d been asleep for all the most important moments of her life, and here the vindictive universe wanted to submerge her one last time, before she could finally— finally —see, before she could finally breathe the same air Agatha had been breathing for centuries. She wouldn’t let it close in on her just yet.

Powered by that rage, she ripped a tear in her own dream, raked across the fabric of reality with only her fingernails, and swam through it. Seeing that Rio was awake, and her pulse still beating, two silent tears of relief slipped down Agatha’s face. She made no effort to wipe them away. She just stared at her with red-rimmed pupils, refusing to blink, as if Rio might disappear if she looked away.

“Rio,” she breathed finally, voice trembling. “I thought you — I thought it was all over.”

Rio smiled at her sadly, tragically, painful adoration squeezing her chest. She wanted to say I’m not going anywhere , I’m here, I’m alive . She wanted to say I see you now and I’ll never forget again, but instead what came out was “And see of never you again.”

At least part of it.

She wondered what that meant. 

She wondered if it had something to do with the word mine.

She wondered, not for the first time that day, how Agatha really felt about her.

Because the visions had been simultaneously both damning and confusing. Because yes Agatha wanted to kiss her, and fuck her, tenderly even. She wanted all of that over the course of several lifetimes, and had it been taken from her time and time again, producing a skeptical madness that Rio could easily understand. 

But did Agatha want to hold her? Even if the magic had played no part—did Agatha want to press against her even when Rio was weak, and tired, and had nothing left to offer?

Did Agatha want to be in love with her? Or was her mind protecting her from exactly that?

Oh. A pit turned in Rio’s stomach.  She’s still scared I’m going to hate her.

“Nothing wrong. Want,” Rio said, then blinked several times as she struggled with the rest of the words. They felt like puddy on her tongue. She made a pathetic little gesture to the space in the bed next to her and resorted to a soft, “Please.”

Agatha’s eyes drifted to the mattress, then back to Rio. Rio steeled herself for a no. She wouldn’t take it personally, not really—Agatha said she had to call Wanda. 

Wanda, who was Agatha’s friend, apparently, despite previous very earnest plans to murder her. That was somehow the least confusing part of all this, even if it was still dizzyingly bizarre. 

After a moment, Agatha shrugged, in apparent defeat. “Okay.”

Rio’s heart leapt in her chest when Agatha crested around the bed, gingerly pulled up the sheets, and slunk in next to her. The other woman had already changed into more comfortable clothes, a navy blue hoodie—one Rio had bought her—and star-patterned sleep bottoms—that Rio had bought her.

One left. Rio could read into the implicit confession. That last memory was Bucharest.

A stronger version of herself would have pried. 

But right now she was only a soft and needy animal.

“Agatha,” Rio said throatily, saying her name correctly for the first time that day. Her entire body was thrumming, having her this close, but not holding her. “Closer. Please.”

The memories had left her with a need to comfort the other woman that was so deep it felt like a chasm inside of Rio’s chest. 

Rio saw a shiver travel down Agatha’s back at her instructions. The other woman didn’t respond for a moment, laying eerily still, before she slowly turned her head, then her whole body, so she was facing Rio. 

Her breaths were slightly labored, her eyes glassy. 

She was crying.

“Promise.”

Now that I understand why, I’m ready for whatever’s coming.

“I know,” Rio laughed, feeling herself fading. “With you, nothing ever is.”

Chapter 27

Chapter Summary

We go back to Bucharest. Also, Agatha texts an old friend.

Rio slept for twenty consecutive hours. This was insane for many reasons. 

Firstly because vampires don’t actually need to sleep, and secondly because Agatha stuck next to her the entire time, right up until the final hour, when her foot had fallen so deeply asleep that she thought she was going to have to saw it off her body.

As the digital clock at her bedside flashed 4:00am, Agatha gently peeled Rio’s arm off her middle, trying her best not to wake her in the process. The vampire responded by hugging her closer. Agatha grunted, Rio’s enormous strength making the innocent gesture feel like she was being tugged backward by a garbage compressor, and she was the garbage.

Apt metaphor.

Garbage. That was pretty much how she felt. Physically. Emotionally. She felt like her soul had been beaten with a baseball bat and then stepped on by those metal-spike boots humans wore in the winter. Those shoes were so ugly. And unnecessary. Was it really that hard to have a sense of balance? Agatha enjoyed walking full-speed in six inch stilettos on the ice just to prove this point every winter. She was regularly on the local news for it. 

Thinking about humans and their embarrassing little ice-poking instruments, Agatha eventually managed to wiggle out of Rio’s grip, feeling both relief and a crushing sadness once she was free of it.

 Standing up and shaking out her limbs, Agatha caught a view of herself in her standing mirror—and froze in place when she noticed Rio’s handprint red and swollen against her stomach. 

She had been holding her so tightly, it’d left a mark.

Agatha wanted to scream. 

…But she didn’t want to wake Rio.

If that wasn’t a perfect description of her life, she didn’t know what else was—fury so hot in her chest that she could tear her own heart out, stamp on it with ice-spikes, but she didn’t. She didn’t because she didn’t want to scare the one single precious thing she had.

So she plucked her phone off the nightstand, and walked out of the room. I really need a new case she thought as she looked at the long crack running from top to bottom. You might never see Rio again after today, and you’re worried about a new case , she thought afterward. Please for the love of god give me a moment of peace, she instructed her insufferable mind, slouching on the couch and flying to Amazon, because that’s what she did when she wanted to avoid worrisome thoughts and inevitabilities—online shopping.

Almost twelve separate cases made it into her cart before that bubbling sensation in the back of her skull got too loud, and she was forced to deal with reality.

She groaned audibly, just loud enough to feel better but not enough to wake Rio, and opened iMessage.

Thumbing over to Ginger Dracula, she bit down on her lip when she saw half a dozen messages were already waiting for her. She ignored them all except the last one.

ginger dracula: Effect’s not going to last much longer, I’m almost out of radius. I delayed Richard as much as I could but he’s insistent we go back to Florida. Is your thrall good yet?

Is your thrall good yet. Agatha almost bit the phone. This woman was so—

agatha: remind me to actually let rio kill you next time.

The truth was, she wasn’t sure Rio could kill Wanda. Or even really injure her. Agatha was absolutely going to have let her try—it would be her equivalent of whatever men felt at a sports game—but she had good money that Wanda would have wiggled out of it just like she always did.

ginger dracula: i’ll take that as a yes, then. 

ginger dracula: so are we going to talk about the fact that you just tried to steal my family fortune from underneath my feet, or are you going to block me again?

She was going to get Richard Ashcroft’s fucking number, and she was going to ride that golden ticket all the way up to the top of the vampire foodchain.

An hour later, Rio woke up to the sound of Agatha loudly washing dishes in the sink.

Which was a weird sound to hear, because they had a dishwasher that Agatha swore by religiously. In fact, ever since the invention of it in 1850, Agatha never laid her hand on a sponge again— they’re festering with germs, Rio— even though Rio kept one by the kitchen sink.

“Agatha?” Rio called out.

She felt a pit of disappointment roll around in her stomach that Agatha hadn’t been lying beside her when she woke up. But Rio couldn’t exactly blame her. She had no idea how long she’d been out for. A quick glance at the clock said it had to be hours—maybe days.

She had never blacked out like that before—so completely, so dreamlessly, just a long black window of nothing. She wondered if that was how mortals slept. Like rocks, hovering near death.

“Coming, coming,” Agatha said in a huff.

When Agatha finally ambled into the room, she was wearing a soap-soaked apron, her hair high in a messy bun, and her cheeks tinted an adorable red, like she’d been really exerting herself. 

“Hi,” Rio laughed. No, giggled. Like a schoolgirl with a crush. Giggled like whatever she and Agatha had was silly and simple, and not ancient and insane. “What on Earth are you doing?”

“What does it look like I’m doing?”

Rio pushed her tongue into her cheek. “Losing your mind?”

Agatha grumbled, “Close. Dishes. ”

“Come here and tell me about it?”

Agatha swallowed, seeming to consider the offer, then with a dramatic sigh she began to force the apron over shoulders, struggling adorably when she got stuck mid-way. 

“Stupid fucking—” She jumped up and down. “ Dumb ass piece of—”

Rio felt her cheeks warm at her closeness. “Didn’t know what to do without me?”

Agatha opened one eye and still managed to glare at her.

Then she paused, and her cheeks grew even more pink, like she’d thought something embarrassing. “Or, more likely, I was thinking of all the ways I could get out of finishing the unwinding process. So you could just forget all this happened so we’d never have to speak about it again. But then I realized I could, technically, do that. By Suggesting you. And then I realized I’m a fucking asshole. And then I found myself doing dishes out of some sort of guilty consciousness. So yes, I’m going insane.”

Rio’s heart broke and swelled at the same time.

Agatha looked away. “Sometimes pigs fly.”

Rio gave her a small, knowing smile.  “I already told you I’m not going to be upset. Whatever it is. Even if you tried to murder me in my sleep.”
